        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer218728925" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10887596957979064300">It's a slightly strange genre, these giant histories of the present. Comparing to the other two i've read - <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/185540.The_Fate_of_Africa_A_History_of_Fifty_Years_of_Independence' title='The Fate of Africa A History of Fifty Years of Independence by Martin Meredith' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Fate of Africa: A History of Fifty Years of Independence</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/29658.Postwar_A_History_of_Europe_Since_1945' title='Postwar A History of Europe Since 1945 by Tony Judt' rel='nofollow noopener'>Postwar: A History of Europe Since 1945</a> - this one takes the cake for sheer human scope: India just has more people than either Europe or Africa. This kind of thing is inevitably always only a skimming of the surface, even if it is 900 pages long. The point would have to be to find some shape to that surface, some grasping to</span>
  <span id="freeText10887596957979064300" style="display:none">It's a slightly strange genre, these giant histories of the present. Comparing to the other two i've read - <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/185540.The_Fate_of_Africa_A_History_of_Fifty_Years_of_Independence" title="The Fate of Africa A History of Fifty Years of Independence by Martin Meredith" rel="nofollow noopener">The Fate of Africa: A History of Fifty Years of Independence</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/29658.Postwar_A_History_of_Europe_Since_1945" title="Postwar A History of Europe Since 1945 by Tony Judt" rel="nofollow noopener">Postwar: A History of Europe Since 1945</a> - this one takes the cake for sheer human scope: India just has more people than either Europe or Africa. This kind of thing is inevitably always only a skimming of the surface, even if it is 900 pages long. The point would have to be to find some shape to that surface, some grasping towards a unified theory of the thing. <br /><br />Guha, however, has the benefit here of working with a continent-sized place which is a single country, so theres an order of magnitude more detail about Indian political history than about any single government in Europe or Africa. This is also kind of the book's downfall though. A political history of modern Indian is - seemingly inevitably - focused on the Congress Party. The Party is inevitably focused on the Nehru-Gandhi dynasty. Despite the massive scope, the book feels a little thin at times. The cast of characters, so to speak, remains relatively limited. <br /><br />Figures like Ambedkar or Vajpayee show up...kind of around the edges. I think - as someone totally unfamiliar with the issues - I would have appreciated more of a follow through of those ideologies and political currents, understanding more of how they interacted with and influenced rule, than another political shenanigan pulled off by Nehru et all. Theres just so much here that merely summing it all up is too much information already, but I still felt that somethng of the grand shape of things, even on the most superficial level, was still out of my sight. Regionalism, communalism, populism, language, caste, religon...they're all brought up and addressed often, but always circling around the straightforward linear narrative. <br /><br />As a total aside, in a chapter about entertainment at the end, he mentions that the Independece movement always had a puritanical streak (and that Gandhi apparently never saw a movie all the way through!) That's fascinating to me. What happened to that? Where did it meet Indian society? How do these things carry through? Not that there isn't a solid effort to get at social history- there is, as well as technological, cultural and economic. All of it is fascinating and none of it is quite enough. <br /><br />Maybe it's because I couldn't help comparing it to Israel at times. The scale is so ridiculously different, and yet there are familiar beats to the broad outline. The dates kind of line up (independence in 47/8, strong socialist centralization, first time opposition taking power in 1977, shifts into capitalism in the 90's along with rises of identity politics and hardening of secterian positions, etc.) and of course the usual questions of religion, communalism, democracy, identity, etc as issues. On the one hand, it kind of makes me feel a little more normal, to think that this is just the way these things play out. On the other hand, it's probably not a very good comparison and sent me looking for patterns that might not exist. <br /><br />If there is a connecting thread that he attempts to follow through, it's the question of democracy on this vast - and varied - a scale. It's amazing to see some of the disdain the very notion of democracy in India was held in at the beginning. Could this many people, unconnected, uneducated, make meaningful political decisions? It jumped out at me, the occassional mentions of groups of people mired in poverty, still, as the years go by, into the 21st century, and I wonder if everything just went over them or if it mattered there. Well, they end up voting for populists and demagogues and sons-of and movie stars. So, they're pretty normal, really. I think the ultimate conclusion, despite Guha's final fairly complex and not unpessimistic assessment of the state of Indian democracy, is that it really, really matters. <br /><br />Very recommended, even if it mostly raised more questions than it answered, for me. <br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10887596957979064300" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_176641264" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/176641264" />
    <a title="M" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1741591-m"><img alt="M" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1360352923p2/1741591.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/176641264?book_show_action=true">Jun 14, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="M" class="user" itemprop="url" name="M" href="/user/show/1741591-m">M</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780060958589" href="/book/show/3519677-india-after-gandhi">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1741591-m?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1741591-m?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1741591-m?shelf=india">india</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1741591-m?shelf=history">history</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer176641264" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10371070836091627622">An excellent, thorough history of modern India, post-independence.<br/><br/>The first half, covering the decades under PM Nehru and the drafting of the Indian Constitution, is really inspiring. Nehru was an idealist who believed in social change; he worked with B.R. Ambedkar, an Untouchable who was the primary draftsman of the constitution, to keep India as a secular state and to overturn the caste system. Nehru also worked to protect minority groups such as women and Muslims, to create an economic and so</span>
  <span id="freeText10371070836091627622" style="display:none">An excellent, thorough history of modern India, post-independence.<br /><br />The first half, covering the decades under PM Nehru and the drafting of the Indian Constitution, is really inspiring. Nehru was an idealist who believed in social change; he worked with B.R. Ambedkar, an Untouchable who was the primary draftsman of the constitution, to keep India as a secular state and to overturn the caste system. Nehru also worked to protect minority groups such as women and Muslims, to create an economic and social system that would enable the poor and underprivileged to improve their circumstances, and to create a truly democratic and united country. He and his home minister, Vallabhbhai Patel, were able to merge diverse people of many languages, religions, and cultures into one democratic nation.<br /><br />In the second half, things begin to crumble. It is ironic that Nehru's own daughter, Indira Gandhi, was able to unravel much of his accomplishments. After her dictatorial reign as PM, India was ensconced in cronyism, nepotism, and identity politics that still plague it today. India now has many political parties, but they focus on simply furthering one group's agenda, based on their caste, religion, province, or otherwise. It is said that in India one doesn't cast a vote, one votes their caste. (My husband says Indians love their pithy puns, but he's one to talk.) <br /><br />Idealism and visionary thinking have been eroded with corruption and single-minded politics. Rather than taking grievances to the courts, or to the paralyzed government system, citizens take to the streets in riots that quickly turn violent. There have been cases where the government has stood by, or even encouraged, mob violence against a particular minority group.<br /><br />Still, India remains a democracy, and for that we must give it credit. One of Guha's central themes is how it has survived as such.<br /><br />Recently a man named Anna Hezare has been in the Indian news. He has been fasting and attracting many followers with his call for the Parliament to pass his proposed anti-corruption bill. He had enough support that Parliament is currently in the process of passing his bill calling for stricter watchdogs on the government. Obviously, such a bill is clearly needed; on the other hand, when will Indians learn to rely on the courts rather than showmanship and mob rioting for desired change? And when will the institutions become something the citizens <i>can</i> rely on?</span>
  <a data-text-id="10371070836091627622" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_212455564" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/212455564" />
    <a title="Anirudh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4784749-anirudh"><img alt="Anirudh" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/212455564?book_show_action=true">Sep 20, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anirudh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anirudh" href="/user/show/4784749-anirudh">Anirudh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4784749-anirudh?shelf=history">history</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer212455564" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14555454814063558399">A good compilation of post independence Indian history by Ramachandra Guha. It also gives a good perspective to understand present day India. The book is rich in detail and the writing style of Guha is brilliant and makes the book a very interesting read. But at a few places Guha does compromise objectivity in favour of Nehru, especially in his telling about Nehru's grand centralized socialist economy and his foreign policy of Non-Aligned movement( India and the Non-Aligned Movement.) both of wh</span>
  <span id="freeText14555454814063558399" style="display:none">A good compilation of post independence Indian history by Ramachandra Guha. It also gives a good perspective to understand present day India. The book is rich in detail and the writing style of Guha is brilliant and makes the book a very interesting read. But at a few places Guha does compromise objectivity in favour of Nehru, especially in his telling about Nehru's grand centralized socialist economy and his foreign policy of Non-Aligned movement( India and the Non-Aligned Movement.) both of which are more or less proven wrong in retrospect(NAM is debatable).<br /><br />In short, the pros of the book –<br /><br />The period between 1947 and 1950, the making of the constitution and the consolidation of the princely states was very well covered. It could have been made better by covering in more detail Ambedkar's contribution,and his story and how he came to be in the Constituent Assembly in the first place. This part of the book makes the whole book worth reading.<br /><br />The reordering of the states on linguistic lines and Potti Sriramulu's battle.<br /><br />The cons of the book are –<br /><br />The author has hardly written about Indian politics since Rajiv Gandhi's time. Guha goes off on a tangent about the role of cricket, Bollywood and other cultural events. Nothing gets spoken about the liberalization of 1991, the effect of the collapse of the Berlin Wall on India, Pokhran nuclear tests, the Bofors scam etc.. The events that take place after 1985 have been given very little space in the book.( I personally think that this was done because it becomes impossible to tell India's tale beyond 1985 without pointing out the many mistakes made by Nehru-Gandhi dynasty, which the author seems very reluctant to do)<br /><br />Should have covered the period of emergency and the Janata Party's first non-congress government that followed in more detail.<br /><br />A certain amount of objectivity would have been good while discussing Nehru's policies and his five year plans in the 1950s and the events that led up to the Indo-China war.<br /><br />Another similar book which I have not read but intend to is Bipin Chandra's India Since Independence. I hear the book covers post 1990 events in more detail, should check it out.<br /><br />But overall the book is well written and definitely worth a read( a couple of reads actually).</span>
  <a data-text-id="14555454814063558399" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1457869834" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1457869834" />
    <a title="Muthu Raj" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41120828-muthu-raj"><img alt="Muthu Raj" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1452406118p2/41120828.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1457869834?book_show_action=true">Dec 02, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Muthu Raj" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Muthu Raj" href="/user/show/41120828-muthu-raj">Muthu Raj</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330505543" href="/book/show/10406417-india-after-gandhi">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Everyone who wants a non-partisan approach to Contemporary India</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41120828-muthu-raj?shelf=owned">owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41120828-muthu-raj?shelf=classy">classy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1457869834" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10714602265401685427">As I set to type this review, I also seriously consider not doing so, on account of my naivete. In all fairness, I am new to this genre and this book had been lying around for more than a year in my shelf, till I started reading it after I had finished some 100 odd pages in a friend's copy.<br/><br/>I have not read any other book that was so dense as this and yet so well-paced. The amount of information in each page is staggering. The only book I know that has more footnotes than this is, perhaps, the Inf</span>
  <span id="freeText10714602265401685427" style="display:none">As I set to type this review, I also seriously consider not doing so, on account of my naivete. In all fairness, I am new to this genre and this book had been lying around for more than a year in my shelf, till I started reading it after I had finished some 100 odd pages in a friend's copy.<br /><br />I have not read any other book that was so dense as this and yet so well-paced. The amount of information in each page is staggering. The only book I know that has more footnotes than this is, perhaps, the Infinite Jest. <br /><br />I started off by watching a TEDx video my Mr.Guha. He was so articulate in his views that I was compelled to read the book immediately. And in his book, he is more articulate, more evocative than I had imagined a writer of history can be.<br />India, is in it's own right, an exception in the world, as a nation. It had not satisfied any condition that major historians and theorists of the day mandated to the formation of a successful, lasting nation. There was no unifying language, no unifying way of life, no absolute majority of a religion. There was only the common theme of being at the receiving end of European colonialism. And that was not enough to forge a united nation, many historians and journalists predicted, at the time, of which Guha is generous to include many samples. <br /><br />One cannot blame those people who predicted the downfall of India as a nation soon after the British left. The Indian populace was illiterate, yet granted adult franchise. A wave of nationalism dominated the first election. It was famously said that 'even a lamp post can win if it contested under Congress auspices'. In essence, India was the first modern <i>complete</i> democracy. It started off as a constitutional one, and has morphed into a populist one.<br /><br />India had its share of wounds too. It was deeply hurting from the communal riots incited as a result of the partition. In fact, the communal violence would continue to dog the country well into the twenty first century, initially condemned by the government and sometimes condoned and actively aided by it, episodically. <br /><br />There was no scarcity for secessionist claims either. It seems like throughout the second half of the century, after the Indian Union came into being, almost every corner of the country wanted a separate nation, every province harbored a desire to be declared a sovereign nation. Domestic terrorism had its share, for good(!) measure.<br /><br />There was the issue of neighbours, their aggression, non pragmatic foreign policies and internal economic policies, all product of the nationalist sentiment that ran high when these were enacted and enforced. And they have all come to pass, with better versions replacing them, albeit slowly, often obstructed by recalcitrant politicians with vested interests and policy makers with Anchor bias and tunneled visions. <br /><br />It is to be noted that, as an (unfortunate) consequence of adopting a democracy, India had to tolerate its politicians. That the literacy rates were below the average of similar democracies / freshly liberated colonies didn't help. India was gifted with great statesmen, who seeded the revolution, nurtured it and lived long enough to see it mature. In the initial decades things were decidedly better than they are now. <br /><br />Since then, India has come a long way. And this book tells you the story of this country. Ramachandra Guha has done an excellent job of chronicling it, without even a hint of distaste towards the politicians or the policy makers or its executives. This is a remarkable achievement in itself.<br /><br />India is the seventh largest country in the world. It is no mean task to select issues that need to be covered, in order to be representative of the government, the nation, and ultimately the populace. However, Mr.Guha proves to be just the man for this task. Nowhere does one feel that some issues were left out, or downplayed, or exaggerated. It must have been very hard to resist those temptations and be non-partisan. Too many historians have claimed to be authoritative re-tellers of the great Indian History. And Mr.Guha shames them all, and not through attacks, but through sheer scholarship.<br /><br />I do not claim that I am enlightened now, even as far as the subject that has been covered in this book. But it is because of my own inability to retain and evaluate all the facts objectively. That I may have to re-read it multiple times to assimilate even the outline of the book is in no doubt. <br /><br />The book is one giant story, sometimes titillating, sometimes depressing and sometimes neither. I made reading this book a ritual, covering a hundred pages in the morning every day. And it has paid off, richly. In part, it also subjected me to much chagrin, at the knowledge of just how much I didn't know about my own country. But Mr.Guha himself vindicates the great nation through his book. For all its imperfections and shortcomings, India has survived this long, and shall survive long enough that it will come to be seen as a country that has always been a democracy.<br /><br />Near the end of the book, the author points out the lamenting of Isaiah Berlin, who pointed out that countries like India shouldn't be seen only as liberated subjects of European colonialism. They have their own distinct history, character, she says. And Mr.Guha's book does justice to that claim. No doubt this will go down as a classic, for it already has become a standard and authoritative text on the subject. <br /><br />Highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10714602265401685427" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review1457869834">
  <td colspan="2">

<span id="freeTextContainer15081086872339723143">A very comprehensive history of modern India, and it's surprisingly interesting and gripping given that it is a historical record. A must read for any one who wants to know how modern India came to be, esp. for those of us who were not born when we were not the fast developing nation we are today. Some of the descriptions and narratives are very transcending. They make you fell nostalgic of an event even when you never were there in the first place. It makes you proud and sad and excited and ash</span>
  <span id="freeText15081086872339723143" style="display:none">A very comprehensive history of modern India, and it's surprisingly interesting and gripping given that it is a historical record. A must read for any one who wants to know how modern India came to be, esp. for those of us who were not born when we were not the fast developing nation we are today. Some of the descriptions and narratives are very transcending. They make you fell nostalgic of an event even when you never were there in the first place. It makes you proud and sad and excited and ashamed all in the same book. In short, it tells you about India, why it is unique and what is good and bad about it, and also what we need to do to improve.<br /><br />It has it's shortcoming and there are points where the author's personal views have influenced what should otherwise have been an objective record (Nehru's regime and later BJP's hindutva) but the sheer length and breadth of the content that Guha has presented here, is just so humbling and detailed. The minor flaws are actually minuscule in the larger picture.</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pranav " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pranav " href="/user/show/39639683-pranav">Pranav </a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1470960273" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9616192177890036938">I don't know what qualities a good history book should possess, I'm no expert. Despite this, this book remains one of the most entertaining reads that I've had a chance to indulge in. <br/><br/>My respect for some of the founding members has grown manifold, independent of whether I agree with their policies. Indian TV and cinema (which by the way, is also touched upon in the book!) has a reputation for dramatisation and sensationalism. I'm pretty sure that they are influenced by some of the amazing gift-o</span>
  <span id="freeText9616192177890036938" style="display:none">I don't know what qualities a good history book should possess, I'm no expert. Despite this, this book remains one of the most entertaining reads that I've had a chance to indulge in. <br /><br />My respect for some of the founding members has grown manifold, independent of whether I agree with their policies. Indian TV and cinema (which by the way, is also touched upon in the book!) has a reputation for dramatisation and sensationalism. I'm pretty sure that they are influenced by some of the amazing gift-of-gab orators that the country seems to produce, without fail. <br /><i><br />‘Lathi goli khayenge, phir bhi Bambai layenge’ - Samyukt Maharashtra Movement<br /><br />‘Wo kehte hain Indira Hatao, hum kehte hain Garibi Hatao’ <br /><br />'All my father's works have been written in prison. I recommend prison life not only for aspiring writers but for aspiring politicians too. - Indira Gandhi<br /><br />'In India, you do not cast your vote, you vote your caste.' - V.N. Gadgil, Congress Politician<br /></i><br />These are some of the many gems you can find in this book. <br /><br />Another amazing aspect of this book is the sources cited. I read this book on my kindle, which showed 66% completed after I was done with the epilogue. The rest of the book is just the sources. Although I haven't had a chance to go down the rabbit hole, just the fact that I can reinforces my belief in the quality of the research that went into it. <br /><br />As the author rightfully points out, no version of history is untainted by the writer's opinion. In spite of, or even with that warning, I think this book has helped me gain great insight into the country that is India, today.<br /></span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/353950877?book_show_action=true">Jun 23, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Viplove Tyagi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Viplove Tyagi" href="/user/show/7928472-viplove-tyagi">Viplove Tyagi</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer282333992835852819">Never has history been told in such colour and with such emotion. Rightly deserves to be called Guha's <i>masterpiece</i>. <br/>A book that takes you through the fight of a young nation against the veritable elements threatening secularism, its dangerous but nevertheless great gamble with democracy, its idealist argument against the more realist one for alignment, its parenthood falling from that of great men of integrity to mortals with vanity, and the rise of populism on the price of constitutional democr</span>
  <span id="freeText282333992835852819" style="display:none">Never has history been told in such colour and with such emotion. Rightly deserves to be called Guha's <i>masterpiece</i>. <br />A book that takes you through the fight of a young nation against the veritable elements threatening secularism, its dangerous but nevertheless great gamble with democracy, its idealist argument against the more realist one for alignment, its parenthood falling from that of great men of integrity to mortals with vanity, and the rise of populism on the price of constitutional democracy. Selflessness and foresight of some, pettiness and fanaticism of some more, revolts for secession, linguist processions, theocratic and socialist movements, poverty and hunger, rights of minorities, even the strange cinema and much more, all find their due description in one volume. <br />With every other statement referenced, one wonders the pain undergone and diligence shown with every book or magazine the author refers, each document that he dusted and each letter that he unearthed. <br />The unnatural idea of an Indian Nation opening the book, and the appreciation of that <i>strange</i> idea being successfully conceived and perpetuated ending it, is sure to fill every democrat's heart with admiration for India, and every nationalist's eyes with tears. <br /><br /><br /></span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1849683205" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1849683205" />
    <a title="Andrew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12798350-andrew"><img alt="Andrew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564684546p2/12798350.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1849683205?book_show_action=true">Jan 07, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrew" href="/user/show/12798350-andrew">Andrew</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780060958589" href="/book/show/3519677-india-after-gandhi">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12798350-andrew?shelf=pakistan">pakistan</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12798350-andrew?shelf=politics-and-political-theory">politics-and-political-theory</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12798350-andrew?shelf=asian-history">asian-history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12798350-andrew?shelf=india">india</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12798350-andrew?shelf=economics-business-finance">economics-business-finance</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1849683205" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14061334255104830370">India After Gandhi: The History of the World's Largest Democracy, by Ramachandra Guha, is a deep and fascinating examination of the world's most populous democracy from the death of Gandhi to 2008. The book begins by examining the fracturing of the Indian National Congress, with Pakistan and India splitting into two nation states. This led to a massive influx of internal refugee movements, and widespread violence between Muslim, Hindu and Sikh communities. It is estimated more than one million p</span>
  <span id="freeText14061334255104830370" style="display:none">India After Gandhi: The History of the World's Largest Democracy, by Ramachandra Guha, is a deep and fascinating examination of the world's most populous democracy from the death of Gandhi to 2008. The book begins by examining the fracturing of the Indian National Congress, with Pakistan and India splitting into two nation states. This led to a massive influx of internal refugee movements, and widespread violence between Muslim, Hindu and Sikh communities. It is estimated more than one million people died in this communal violence throughout India and Pakistan. <br /><br />Mahatma Gandhi was assassinated during this split by a Hindu extremist. Gandhi sorely wished to see India and Pakistan unite as one nation, but his wish was ultimately dashed. His successor in the Congress, Jawaharlal Nehru strove to keep Gandhi's vision going. Nehru was an extremely adept politician, and one of the most widely respected characters in post-independence India. What he inherited were some issues, however. The British had left, but a collection of over 500 Princely States in India remained. It was the Indian governments goal to incorporate these states (and Pakistan's too). <br /><br />Many of these states were small collections of villages, but some were massive, and yearned for independence. Hyperbad was a large state in south central India. It had long existed as a vassal to the Mughal Empire, and this treaty was inherited by the British Raj. After independence, the Maharaja of Hyperbad sought to keep his state independent. He set up armed forces to try and resist Indian occupation, and sought international support for his cause. India sent in thousands of troops and annexed Hyperbad soon after independence. Tranvacore was another valuable Princely state. Situated on the Indian coast, it had an efficient government, contained resources to construct nuclear devices, and had the backing of the British. The leader of Tranvacore also sought independence, but was attacked by Indian nationalists, and in hospital, gave his approval to join India. Kashmir, however, was the sore spot. Kashmir was a strategically important state touching Himalaya mountains. It was largely Muslim, but its ruling class were anti-Pakistan for political reasons. During the trouble of Partition and the subsequent refugee crisis, Kashmir was a bastion of stability. All this changed when tribal groups from Pakistan entered Kashmir to try and force its annexation to Pakistan. India retaliated with troops of its own, and general conflict began. When the dust settled, the area was split between Pakistan and India - the situation that largely exists today. This conflict has yet to be resolved, at the time of writing. <br /><br />Nehru's India was chaotic, and many predicted it would fall to military rule after his death. The internal situation in India was fractured, due to the multitude of religious, ethnic, cultural, language, caste groups, ideologies and so on. The system of regional states within India was difficult, as many individual groups vied for their own independent states. Tribal groups in eastern India, like the Naga and Mizo fought pitched insurgencies (the Naga) for independence, or created political groups to try and create their own states (Jharakand, Mizoram etc.). Gujarati speakers did not want the city of Bombay to join the state of Maharashtra. Sikhs in the Punjab region also wanted their own state. The language of India was also contentious. What should be the language of the political centre and its courts? Some in the southern states cried foul when Hindi was proposed, and wanted the main language to remain English. On top of this, the complex caste system of India created situations of intolerance and favouritism. Those from the untouchable Caste struggled to find employment, and were discriminated against in every day life. Tensions existed between various castes and often flared up into violence. Hindu extremism, characterized by the RSS party, sought to end Indian secularism and create a Hindu state. This too resulted in violence between Hindu and Muslim communities.<br /><br />Nehru balanced all of these issues fairly well, while keeping India on its democratic course. Nehru had little real political challengers during his tenure, as he was Gandhi's chosen successor. Nehru's Congress Party dominated India's political sphere for his 15 years in office. He deftly sidestepped challengers, and was able to hold his own during periods of strife and conflict. He fought hard to keep India from succumbing to Communalism on one had, and Communism on the other. He faced challenges from Hindu Nationalists. He supported state-led developmentalism, moving toward a more Soviet style economy. This had mixed results in India for a number of reasons, but did successfully start India down the road to Industrialization. <br /><br />Nehru's foreign policy situation was also tricky. Originally, Nehru sought warm ties with China, but these ties soured over territorial disputes in the Himalaya's, resulting in war between India and China in the 1960's, which led to the loss of territory in both Eastern India and in Kashmir. China then sought relations with Pakistan. India also had frosty relations with the United States. On the surface, India and the US have much in common. Both are large and successful democracies. Both secularist. However, India's experiments with socialist economics and policies, and the USA's prioritization of relations with Pakistan, and subsequent arms deals soured all attempts. India turned to the Soviet block for support. The falling out between China and the USSR was a key moment for India to modernize its economy and armed forces, and India and the USSR signed treaties of cooperation and friendship. Even still, Nehru sought a middle road, and promoted non-alignment in Asia. <br /><br />Nehru passed away during the Sino-Indian conflict, and was succeeded briefly by another Prime Minister, who also died soon after. His daughter, Indira Gandhi, succeeded to lead the Congress, and would dominat Indian politics for the next 16 years. She saw the annexation of Goa from Portugal, the incorporation of French remnants on the Indian subcontinent, the incorporation of Sikkim, but most importantly, a smashing victory over Pakistan. In 1971, East Pakistan rose up in revolt against West Pakistan. The Bengali's of East Pakistan did not have political representation, and were long abused by Islamabad. India supported Bengali independence with a direct invasion, and helped proclaim the independence of Bangladesh after a brief occupation. These victories were strategically used by Gandhi to stay in power.<br /><br />Indira Gandhi's political and economic moves were more autocratic than her fathers. Gandhi supported a centralization of economics, and derided free trade or liberalization. She sought closer ties still with the Soviets, and helped build up the armed forces of India to become a regional player, and a direct challenge to China. On the political front, she struggled with political representation, and after protests and communal violence erupted throughout the nation, she proclaimed a state of emergency, jailing all political opponents and stifling the press. This state of emergency led to an easing of economic and social tensions, but was deeply unpopular for obvious reasons. Indira seemed set to create a dictatorship in India, and had been promoting her son, Sanjay Gandhi, as her successor. Thankfully, after a year of emergency rule, she suddenly released all political prisoners, and held fresh elections, which she lost to a coalition of Hindu nationalist parties, regional parties, and a defection of older Congress politicians. <br /><br />This coalition would only last three years in power, as it succumbed to internal political tensions, and Indira Gandhi was reelected to power once more. This time in office saw her son Sanjay die in a plane accident, and she promoted her other son Rajiv Gandhi. However, she moved away from her authoritarian past, as she required coalition support to ensure an electoral majority. She chose the communists as a political partner - this cementing her state-run economic ideals. She also went on a charm offensive in the west to promote India's image. During this time, ethnic and religious tensions again flared, resulting in conflicts across the country, and violent communal violence. The worst hit Punjab, the Sikh region of India, where tensions between Sikhs and Hindus - unheard of in the past, flared into extreme violence. An armed uprising of Sikhs looking for political autonomy/independence for their state, was violently put down. In retaliation, two of Gandhi's Sikh bodyguard assassinated her. <br /><br />Her son, Rajiv, was next in line. He saw to an opening of the Indian economy, movement toward liberalization of trade and economics, and to soothe tensions between India's communities. He successfully ended the Sikh insurrection, and the issues with the Mizo and Naga tribes. He sent troops overseas to Sri Lanka to assist that governments conflict with Tamil rebels. He also saw India move toward a more Parliamentary style democracy, with frequent elections, coalition politics and a strengthening of democratic practices - which led to his electoral defeat. In the subsequent election campaign, Rajiv was assassinated by a Tamil extremist. <br /><br />The time after Rajiv is a blur of names and figures. The BJP (as of writing the ruling party in India) took prominence as a collective of Hindu nationalist parties. This party successfully competed with Congress in elections post Rajiv - by this time the Congress had split many times under the Gandhi/Neru political clan. This period (1980's forward) saw many wonderful things happen in India, and many terrible ones too. Literacy, economic well being, women's rights, caste rights, and so on increased markedly. The Indian economy began to open up to more neo-liberal policies, moving away from its experiments in socialism and centralized economic planning. This led to both an increase in GDP per capita and average income, and saw a massive shift in poverty levels toward the middle class. It also saw rising tensions between minority groups, unequal distribution of income, massive environmental damage (ie. Bhopal disaster) and so on. <br /><br />I won't go on any further. But what did I think of the book? India After Gandhi was highly engaging, and offered a deeply analytical look at India's history. It maps the political, internal and external pressures the Indian state has faced during its history. Ethnic tensions and violence continue to be the norm in this massive and varied state, but India continues to be a democracy - defying many Western analysis throughout history. It is slowly building itself to be a powerhouse economy, and is widely thought to be on its way to Great Power status in the future. India is a nation that is not often written about in Western literature, and is often overshadowed by China's economic dominance. Even so, is is with great pride that India remains a stable democracy. It suffers from internal strife, poverty, nepotism (as clearly shown above), corruption and environmental degradation. However, it has strong democratic institutions, and is making great strides in all its endeavors. Guha's book examines all aspects of this nation with a critical eye, offering facts and analysis, and avoids glowing prose in favour of realistic analysis. Highly recommended for those looking to read about India and its incredible journey from colony to colossus. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14061334255104830370" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2268892791" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2268892791" />
    <a title="Shoti" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53373701-shoti"><img alt="Shoti" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1477072777p2/53373701.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2268892791?book_show_action=true">Jun 25, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shoti" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shoti" href="/user/show/53373701-shoti">Shoti</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53373701-shoti?shelf=politics">politics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53373701-shoti?shelf=history-development">history-development</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2268892791" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7986943779308782788">The heterogeneity of India is truly baffling. What is that common denominator that may unite hundreds of millions of Indians into one nation? Perhaps it is religion as in the case of Pakistan? No, it cannot be religion. Though the majority of Indians are Hindu the country has the second largest population of Muslims in the world in addition to substantial communities of Buddhists, Christians, Jains, Sikhs. What about having a common language? Nice try but that doesn’t work either. The Constituti</span>
  <span id="freeText7986943779308782788" style="display:none">The heterogeneity of India is truly baffling. What is that common denominator that may unite hundreds of millions of Indians into one nation? Perhaps it is religion as in the case of Pakistan? No, it cannot be religion. Though the majority of Indians are Hindu the country has the second largest population of Muslims in the world in addition to substantial communities of Buddhists, Christians, Jains, Sikhs. What about having a common language? Nice try but that doesn’t work either. The Constitution of India recognizes no fewer than twenty-two different official languages. Ok, then perhaps equality of wealth, or the lack of it, as in a socialist country? No, there’s a huge disparity between the wealthy and the poor in India. The severely polarizing caste system implying harsh discrimination against Dalits / tribal people / other backward castes and the strong discrimination by gender make this puzzle even more mind-boggling. No wonder that politicians / economists / leading columnists have constantly been speculating about the disintegration of India. In defiance of doomsayers India not only survived as a united country but also had a series of democratic parliamentary and state elections since Independence. That is no mean feat and India is a refreshing exception to the big majority of former colonies which shortly after gaining independence degenerated into militant / communist / sectarian regimes. No need to look further than Pakistan. Pakistan kicked off its independent life simultaneously with India, upon Partition, but ended up as an Islamic state which is often governed by army generals instead of democratically elected politicians. Or China which is similarly populous but far less divided on ethnic or religious line and far less poor. Still, a book describing the history of free democratic elections organized in modern China would make a rather short read...<br /><br />In his monumental work Ramachandra Guha provides a summary of the bittersweet story of modern India. It’s inspiring to read about brilliant Indian statesmen like Nehru, Patel or Ambedkar who laid the foundations of a democratic, progressive, secular state. Pacifying ethnic and religious conflicts, integrating the princely states, tolerating ambitions for linguistic independence of states within the Union, keeping the Indian Army out of politics, striving to abolish discrimination based on caste / gender / religion have paved the way for modern India. Probably most importantly, the political leadership realized early on that India has a standing chance of long-term survival only if it can successfully deal with its heterogeneity without enforcing the will of the majority onto any minority. In that regard, the huge diversity prevailing along many axes within India might have fermented rather than restrained democratic sentiment and politics. Guha highly praises the founding fathers and India seems to have been tremendously lucky to have such determined and selfless politicians at the helm during its infancy as an independent state. They lived ‘for’ politics rather than living ‘off’ politics. That does not hold true for most of their successors though and the current Indian democracy is certainly not without blemishes. The country must overcome tough challenges such as widespread corruption and nepotism in politics, frequent conflagration of ethnic and sectarian conflicts, environmental degradation, emergence of Hindu nationalism or the yet prevailing discrimination based on caste / gender / religion - if not under the Constitution then in everyday life. Considering that modern history of India constitutes the modern history to one-sixth of humankind one could only hope that the heart-and-soul approach of the Indian people and the democratic traditions already deeply entrenched in the Indian electorate will keep the country on its path as the most populous democracy in the world, just as it has been since 1947.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7986943779308782788" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_259631535" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/259631535" />
    <a title="Nikunj" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7378481-nikunj"><img alt="Nikunj" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1326118077p2/7378481.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/259631535?book_show_action=true">Jan 10, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nikunj" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nikunj" href="/user/show/7378481-nikunj">Nikunj</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer259631535" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12874979678975938250">For us Indians, the history usually end with the Independence Day i.e. Aug 15, '47 or more specifically when <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4467789.Mahatma_Gandhi' title='Mahatma Gandhi' rel='nofollow noopener'>Mahatma Gandhi</a> died on Oct 30, '48. But what about the events after that, that rocked us and shaped our destiny, of what and who we are today. The greatest experiment in democracy deserve an equal credit to share its story of ups and downs and what it has become as of today.<br/><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/356824.India_After_Gandhi_The_History_of_the_World_s_Largest_Democracy' title='India After Gandhi The History of the World's Largest Democracy by Ramachandra Guha' rel='nofollow noopener'>India After Gandhi</a> is the book that bridges this gap by providing a first rate account of the various people that pa</span>
  <span id="freeText12874979678975938250" style="display:none">For us Indians, the history usually end with the Independence Day i.e. Aug 15, '47 or more specifically when <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4467789.Mahatma_Gandhi" title="Mahatma Gandhi" rel="nofollow noopener">Mahatma Gandhi</a> died on Oct 30, '48. But what about the events after that, that rocked us and shaped our destiny, of what and who we are today. The greatest experiment in democracy deserve an equal credit to share its story of ups and downs and what it has become as of today.<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/356824.India_After_Gandhi_The_History_of_the_World_s_Largest_Democracy" title="India After Gandhi The History of the World's Largest Democracy by Ramachandra Guha" rel="nofollow noopener">India After Gandhi</a> is the book that bridges this gap by providing a first rate account of the various people that participated in experiment. Their expectations and impact with the democracy are very beautifully told as well. <br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/67629.Ramachandra_Guha" title="Ramachandra Guha" rel="nofollow noopener">Ramachandra Guha</a> has beautifully woven all the pieces of the story and the result is a phenomenal book. Scoring high on facts, the language is simple yet powerful. He has tried to maintain an unbiased purview while narrating the incidents. But that doesn't stops him to quote major historians, key figures and other such intelligentsia of the time to quote what their views and prophecies are and how it actually turns out to be.<br /><br />Overall, a must read for everyone, for this story answers the question very well that inspite of all the odds why India still stills survives. The following timeless lines by Ghalib very well express this dilemma:<br /><i> Said I one night to a pristine seer<br />(Who knows the secrets of the whirling Time)<br />'Sir, you well perceive,<br />That Goodness and faith,<br />Fidelity and love<br />Have all departed from this sorry land.<br />Father and son are at each other's throats;<br />Brother fights Brother. Unity<br />and Federation are undetermined.<br />Despite these ominous signs<br />Why has not doomsday come?<br />Why does not the Last Trumpet sound?<br />Who holds the reins of final catastrophe? </i><br /><br />For the answer received by him in poem as well as the actual answer which is told in the story, you will have to read this book and I bet you won't regret it.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12874979678975938250" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2233808207" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2233808207" />
    <a title="Radhika Roy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10083565-radhika-roy"><img alt="Radhika Roy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1509314607p2/10083565.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2233808207?book_show_action=true">Oct 04, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Radhika Roy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Radhika Roy" href="/user/show/10083565-radhika-roy">Radhika Roy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789382616979" href="/book/show/35700249-india-after-gandhi">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2233808207" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10330901923406634187">What a terrific repository of information. Now I understand why it’s a recommended reading for a Civil Services aspirant because this book, while wonderfully covering the length and breadth of the topic at hand, is as general as it can get with regard to the socio-political as well as the economical events that have taken place in the 70 years since our Independence. <br/><br/>Encompassing decades of history, Guha’s magnum opus takes a reader through events that transpired right before India became sovere</span>
  <span id="freeText10330901923406634187" style="display:none">What a terrific repository of information. Now I understand why it’s a recommended reading for a Civil Services aspirant because this book, while wonderfully covering the length and breadth of the topic at hand, is as general as it can get with regard to the socio-political as well as the economical events that have taken place in the 70 years since our Independence. <br /><br />Encompassing decades of history, Guha’s magnum opus takes a reader through events that transpired right before India became sovereign, up to Modi’s reign in September 2016. Sadly, this leaves out Guha’s views on demonetisation (November 2016) which would have been quite interesting to read. <br /><br />First and foremost, the author’s bias towards Nehru is palpable throughout the pages, and for a good reason. He’s full of praises for our first Prime Minister, exhibits tempered criticism towards Indian National Congress, and harbours utter disgust towards BJP-RSS and its ilk. <br /><br />As I agree with his views, I am not inclined to criticise the same. Though I would say that as a historian, an objective perspective would have been preferable. His preference of INC is what made the political party promote him extensively and even award him the Padma Bhushan in 2009 (the book was first published in 2007). <br /><br />Secondly, the book chronicles the conflicts that have ravaged the nation, from the Partition to the onslaught on freedom of speech and expression under BJP, and attempts to portray to our readers how India, despite all odds, has braved the constant stormy weather to maintain the skeleton of a democracy (even if it’s “phiphty-phiphty”). <br /><br />He touches upon issues of caste-based violence, religious fundamentalism, conflicts with tribals, women empowerment, economic development, environmental degradation, language barriers, wars with Pakistan/China, relation with US and many, many more layers that are a part of the Indian society. <br /><br />One area that I feel should have merited a bit more elaboration is the state of the judiciary. Guha of course mentions the judiciary’s abdication of its duty during the Emergency, and sometimes refers to it while speaking of broader topics such as the Shah Bano case, but overall, a reading of this book gives the view that the judiciary has not played too big a role in the country’s development and has always been subservient to the other two pillars of democracy. <br /><br />Thirdly, while I concede that this is a must read for anyone who wishes to understand why the Indian society functions the way it does and what are the reasons for the problems that we are faced with today, I think it needs to be supplemented with extraneous reading material. Though I must say, I cannot imagine the amount of research that must have gone into the writing of this tome. <br /><br />I would recommend this to everyone. Barring the size, it is quite an easy-read and is very interesting, specially if you’re into history. I’m glad I picked it up because it made me realise that my knowledge of post-Independence India was terribly inadequate. <br /><br />PS: In case anyone’s interested, do pick up Madhav Khosla’s India’s Founding Moment. It gives a deeper and more comprehensive insight into what made our Founding Fathers choose parliamentary democracy, even though predictions of doom were voiced from all corners of the world.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10330901923406634187" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3532949833" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3532949833" />
    <a title="Vinayak Mishra" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21013035-vinayak-mishra"><img alt="Vinayak Mishra" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484403135p2/21013035.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3532949833?book_show_action=true">Sep 27, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vinayak Mishra" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vinayak Mishra" href="/user/show/21013035-vinayak-mishra">Vinayak Mishra</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3532949833" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13187946910512283710">India After Gandhi is principally a political history - Ramchandra Guha covers the major events that occurred in India between 1947 - 2000s and their impact on the political landscape. He puts in great effort to add perspectives from a variety of sources - letters, memoirs, biographies, news reports and even his personal experiences - to ensure that we understand what was going through the minds of not only those in power, but also those fighting to oppose them.<br/><br/>The book is written mostly in chro</span>
  <span id="freeText13187946910512283710" style="display:none">India After Gandhi is principally a political history - Ramchandra Guha covers the major events that occurred in India between 1947 - 2000s and their impact on the political landscape. He puts in great effort to add perspectives from a variety of sources - letters, memoirs, biographies, news reports and even his personal experiences - to ensure that we understand what was going through the minds of not only those in power, but also those fighting to oppose them.<br /><br />The book is written mostly in chronological order (~80% of it) and broken into parts - the foundation of the new democracy (pre-independence-early 1950s), Nehru's first two terms in power (1950s-early 1960s) , the tumultuous late 60s, the rise of populism (1970s-late 1980s) and the a topic wise coverage of recent history (1990s-2000s). Throughout the book the author also sheds light on Indian society and social movements as well, but mostly only to add color to the political situation.<br /><br /><u> What I liked about this book: </u><br />1. <b> The narrative </b> - this book is 750+ pages, but impossible to put down! The author makes most of the book one comprehensive story, which is so hard to do when writing about the general history of an entire country. Naturally, this means sacrificing on breadth of topics covered, but I would personally prefer rich detail over lip-service to variety for the sake of it.<br /><br />2. <b> The author's attempt at explaining "Why India survives" </b> - Ramchandra Guha compares and contrasts India with other nations, both present and historical, and explains how India is a "new" concept on the World stage. Western journalists were quick to predict how India succumb to the allure of an dictatorship to maintain order amongst such a diverse populace, but how, ironically it is our tolerance of different sub-cultures that enables us to survive as one nation. He details the ideas of different movements and political factions in a relatively unbiased manner and it's hard to escape the fact that today we're moving closer and closer to the kind of mindset that the founders of our nation feared would be our undoing.<br /><br /><u> What I disliked </u><br />1. <b> Coverage of the recent past </b>: I feel the strength of this book is it's narrative, and the author sacrifices it for the present period for the sake of objectivity. Instead of continuing in a linear fashion, he goes topic by topic, writing about Rights, Rulers, Riots, Riches and Entertainment. It's hard to read through this section without feeling disappointed - the section feels aimless and is missing the diversity of perspective present in the other parts of the book.<br /><br />I've always been very interested in history and considered it a shame that I didn't know enough about the contemporary history of my own country till now. I'm glad I read this book (thanks Kritin!)</span>
  <a data-text-id="13187946910512283710" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2334602232" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2334602232" />
    <a title="Aditya Kulkarni" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/66004125-aditya-kulkarni"><img alt="Aditya Kulkarni" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1489687278p2/66004125.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2334602232?book_show_action=true">Apr 13, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aditya Kulkarni" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aditya Kulkarni" href="/user/show/66004125-aditya-kulkarni">Aditya Kulkarni</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/35683487-india-after-gandhi">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/66004125-aditya-kulkarni?shelf=indian-history">indian-history</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2334602232" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12809221011820996767">First of all, I would like to thank Ramachandra Guha for writing this book. There are very few books on the subject, i.e. the general contemporary history of India or the history of India since independence and the sheer content of the book, the variety of topics it covers is indeed worth appreciating. I really like the extent to which the author has covered the North Eastern part of the country which is quite rare because most the North East is one of the most neglected parts by authors, politi</span>
  <span id="freeText12809221011820996767" style="display:none">First of all, I would like to thank Ramachandra Guha for writing this book. There are very few books on the subject, i.e. the general contemporary history of India or the history of India since independence and the sheer content of the book, the variety of topics it covers is indeed worth appreciating. I really like the extent to which the author has covered the North Eastern part of the country which is quite rare because most the North East is one of the most neglected parts by authors, politicians, and historians alike. It was refreshing to see Mr Guha cover this particular part of India with reasonable detail. The chapter that deals with the emergency of 1975-1977 is simply brilliant and reveals the scholarship of the author. Anecdotes throughout the book reveal the extensive research done by the author and his eye for details. <br /><br />The author is a known worshipper of Jawaharlal Nehru and the high praise for him is a constant throughout the book even while dealing with the matters of the 1990s and early 2000s. Even Atal Bihari Vajpayee is described as a Hindu leader who admired Nehru. I found this constant to be amusing to be honest. First instance where I felt disappointed was in the chapter that deals with the reorganisation of states. The author has covered the unification movements of Andhra, Maharashtra, and Punjab in great detail, providing anecdotes and extensive details regarding the leading figures of the respective movements. However, he has conveniently ignored the Karnataka Ekikarana movement which has a history of more than 100 years and the name of Aluru Venkata Rao is nowhere mentioned in the account. Considering the fact that the author has been residing in Karnataka from decades and has been embraced by the state as one of its own, even with the Rajyotsava award bestowed upon him last year, it was very disappointing for me as a Kannadiga.<br /><br />The second instance that disappoints me is that while describing the 1971 war with Pakistan which led to the creation of Bangladesh, Mr Guha does not even mention the name of Field Marshal Sam Manekshaw who was the chief of the Indian army at this point of time and is widely hailed as one of Indian army's greatest ever officers. Even while describing Field Marshal Cariappa in his epilogue, Mr Guha is quite critical about the great man who was nicknamed as Kipper and was the first Indian chief of Indian army. <br /><br />When dealing with issues such as majority vs minority, the lapsed Marxist in the author resurfaces and conveniently takes the side of the minority on almost every occasion. What is disappointing here is that the author hasn't looked at both sides of the story. His sources are also one dimensional in this regard. Every story has two sides to it and the author hasn't really looked at both the sides of the story when it comes to this aspect.<br /><br />However, the most disappointing part of the book is the description of the Gujarat riots of 2002. While describing the incident that led to the riots, the author literally dismisses the murder of 58 souls who were burnt alive by a frenzied mob at Godhra as an accident. He claims that as per the forensics and police investigation that was done, it was ruled that it was an accident. He does not discuss about the multiple commissions that were formed to look into it, especially the Nanavati-Shah commission which was the main one in this case which investigated the complex case from various perspectives and finally arrived at the conclusion that it was a pre planned conspiracy and the findings of the commission to a large extent were upheld by the trial court which declared that it was indeed a criminal conspiracy. The author doesn't mention any of these points and his sources regarding this are also known to be biased individuals such as Siddharth Varadarajan and Rana Ayyub.<br /><br />The author is known for strong views against the Hindu right wing in India and quite naturally the narrative is quite biased against the organisations that fall under this category. Another aspect which was quite amusing is the coverage given to Kashmir throughout the book. I felt that it was too much to be honest and a minor provincial leader like Sheikh Abdullah gets too much coverage than what is required and more than some of the national leaders who were the founding members of the Indian republic. <br /><br />Overall the book is a mix of all kinds of things. It is brilliant in some aspects, good in other aspects, bad in some parts, and ugly in other parts. What works in the book's favour is its exhaustive, comprehensive content and the lack of viable alternatives when it comes to the general contemporary history of India or the history of India after independence to the present day.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12809221011820996767" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
